history of china 1738 there be few nation in the world more talk of or less known than the chinese the confused and imperfect account which traveller have give of they grandeur they science and they policy have hitherto excite admiration but have not be sufficient to satisfy even a superficial curiosity i therefore return you my thanks for have undertake at so great a expense to convey to english reader the most copious and accurate account yet publish of that remote and celebrated people whose antiquity magnificence power wisdom peculiar custom and excellent constitution undoubtedly deserve the attention of the publick as the satisfaction find in read description of distant country arise from a comparison which every reader naturally make between the idea which he receive from the relation and those which be familiar to he before or in other word between the country with which he be acquaint and that which the author display to he imagination so it vary accord to the likeness or dissimilitude of the manners of the two nation any custom or law unheard and unthought of before strike we with that surprise which be the effect of novelty but a practice conformable to we own please we because it flatter we self-love by show we that we opinion be approve by the general concurrence of mankind of these two pleasure the first be more violent the other more last the first seem to partake more of instinct than reason and be not easily to be explain or define the latter have its foundation in good sense and reflection and evidently depend on the same principle with most human passion a attentive reader will frequently feel each of these agreeable emotion in the perusal of du halde he will find a calm peaceful satisfaction when he read the moral precept and wise instruction of the chinese sage he will find that virtue be in every place the same and will look with new contempt on those wild reasoner who affirm that morality be merely ideal and that the distinction between good and ill be wholly chimerical but he will enjoy all the pleasure that novelty can afford when he become acquaint with the chinese government and constitution he will be amaze to find that there be a country where nobility and knowledge be the same where man advance in rank as they advance in learning and promotion be the effect of virtuous industry where no man think ignorance a mark of greatness or laziness the privilege of high birth he surprise will be still heighten by the relation he will there meet with of honest minister who however incredible it may seem have be see more than once in that monarchy and have adventure to admonish the emperor of any deviation from the law of they country or any error in they conduct that have endanger either they own safety or the happiness of they people he will read of emperor who when they have be address in this manner have neither storm nor threaten nor kick they minister nor think it majestick to be obstinate in the wrong but have with a greatness of mind worthy of a chinese monarch bring they action willingly to the test of reason law and morality and scorn to exert they power in defense of that which they could not support by argument i must confess my wonder at these relation be very great and have be much greater have i not often entertain my imagination with a instance of the like conduct in a prince of england on a occasion that happen not quite a century ago and which i shall relate that so remarkable a example of spirit and firmness in a subject and of conviction and compliance in a prince may not be forget and i hope you will look upon this letter as intend to do honor to my country and not to serve you interest by promote you undertake the prince at the christening of he first son have appoint a noble duke to stand as proxy for the father of the princess without regard to the claim of a marquis -lrb- heir apparent to a higher title -rrb- to whom as lord of the bedchamber then in wait that honor properly belong -- the marquis be wholly unacquainted with the affair till he hear at dinner the duke 's health drunk by the name of the prince he be that evening to represent this he take a opportunity after dinner of inquire the reason of and be inform by the prince 's treasurer of he highness 's intention the marquis immediately declare that he think he right invade and he honor injure which he could not bear without require satisfaction from the usurper of he privilege nor would he longer serve a prince who pay no regard to he lawful pretension the treasurer could not deny that the marquis 's claim be incontestable and by he permission acquaint the prince with he resolution the prince thereupon send for the marquis demand with a resentful and imperious air how he could dispute he command and by what authority he presume to control he in the management of he own family and the christening of he own son the marquis answer that he do not encroach upon the prince 's right but only defend he own : that he think he honor concerned and as he be a young man would not enter the world with the loss of he reputation the prince exasperate to a very high degree repeat he command but the marquis with a spirit and firmness not to be depress or shake persist in he determination to assert he claim and conclude with declare that he would do himself the justice that be deny he and that not the prince himself should trample on he character he be then order to withdraw and the duke come to he assure he that the honor be offer he unasked that when he accept it he be not inform of he lordship 's claim and that now he very willingly resign it the marquis very gracefully acknowledge the civility of the duke 's expression and declare himself satisfied with he grace 's conduct but think it inconsistent with he honor to accept the representation as a cession of the duke or on any other term than as he own acknowledge right the prince be inform of the whole conversation and have upon inquiry find all the precedent on the marquis 's side think it below he dignity to persist in a error and restore the marquis to he right upon he own condition continue he in he favor believe the midnight and with tea welcome the morning he begin by refute a popular notion that bohea and green tea be leave of the same shrub gather at different time of the year he be of opinion that they be produce by different shrub the leaf of tea be gather in dry weather then dry and curl over the fire in copper pan the chinese use little green tea imagine that it hinder digestion and excite fever how which experience do not confirm when the chinese drink tea they infuse it slightly and extract only the more volatile part but though this seem to require great quantity at a time yet the author believe perhaps only because he have a inclination to believe it that the english and dutch use more than all the inhabitant of that extensive empire the chinese drink it sometimes with acid seldom with sugar and this practice we author who have no intention to find anything right at home recommend to he countryman the history of the rise and progress of tea-drinking be truly curious tea be begin to use green tea and the practice of drink it descend to the lower class of the people in 1720 the french begin to send it hither by a clandestine commerce from 1717 to 1726 we import annually seven hundred thousand pound from 1732 to 1742 a million and two hundred thousand pound be every year bring to london in some year afterwards three million and in 1755 near four million of pound or two thousand ton in which we be not to reckon that which be surreptitiously introduce which perhaps be nearly as much such quantity be indeed sufficient to alarm we it be at least worth inquiry to begin however by question the virtue ascribe to it and deny that the crew of the chinese ship be preserve in they voyage homewards from the scurvy by tea about this report i have make some inquiry and though i can not find that these crew be wholly exempt from scorbutick malady they seem to suffer they less than other mariner in any course of equal length this i ascribe to the tea not as possess any medicinal quality but as tempting they to drink more water to dilute they salt food more copiously and perhaps to forbear punch or other strong liquor he then proceed in the pathetick strain to with a weak digestion low spirit lassitude melancholy and twenty disorder which in spite of the faculty have yet no name except the general one of nervous complaint let they change they diet and among other article leave off drinking tea it be more than probable the greatest part of they will be restore to health hot water be also very hurtful to the tooth the chinese do not drink they tea so hot as we do and yet they have bad tooth this can not be ascribe entirely to sugar for they use very little as already observe but we all know that hot or cold thing which pain the tooth destroy they also if we drink less tea and use gentle acid for the gum and tooth particularly sour orange though we have a less number of french dentist i fancy this essential part of beauty would be much better preserve the woman in the united province who sip tea from morning till night be also as remarkable for bad tooth they also look pallid and many be trouble with certain feminine disorder arise from a relax habit the portuguese lady on the other hand entertain with sweetmeat and yet they have very good tooth but they food in general be more of a farinaceous and vegetable kind than ours they also drink cold water instead of sip hot and never taste any fermented liquor for these reason the use of sugar do not seem to be at all pernicious to they man seem to have lose they stature and comeliness and woman they beauty i be not young but methink there be not quite so much beauty in this land as there be you very chambermaid have lose they bloom i suppose by sip tea even the agitation of the passion at card be not so great enemy to female charm what shakespeare ascribe to the concealment of love be in this age more frequently occasion by the use of tea to raise the fright still higher he quote a account of a pig 's tail scald with tea on which however he do not much insist of these dreadful effect some be perhaps imaginary and some may have another cause that there be less beauty in frequent than in any former time be i believe true however deplorable but this new race of evil will not be expel by the prohibition of tea this general languor be the effect of general luxury of review -lrb- 7 -rrb- of a essay on the writings and genius of pope this be a very curious and entertaining miscellany of critical remark and literary history though the book promise nothing but observation on the writings of pope yet no opportunity be neglect of introduce the character of any other writer or the mention of any performance or event in which learning be interested from pope however he always take he hint and to pope he return again from he digression the fact which he mention though they be seldom anecdote in a rigorous sense be often such as be very little known and such as will delight more reader than naked criticism as he examine the work of this great poet in a order nearly chronological he necessarily begin with he pastoral which consider as representation of any kind of life he very justly censure for there be in they a mixture of grecian and english of ancient and modern image windsor be couple with hyblum and thame with pactolus he then compare some passage which pope have imitate or translate with the imitation or version and give the preference to the original perhaps not always upon convincing argument theocritus make he lover wish to be a bee that he might creep among the leaf that form the chaplet of he mistress pope 's enamoured swain long to be make the captive bird that sing in he fair one 's bower that she might listen to he song and reward he with she kiss the critick prefer the image of theocritus as more wild more delicate and more uncommon it be natural for a lover to wish that he might be any thing that could come near to he lady but we more naturally desire to be that which she fondle and caress than that which she would avoid at least would neglect the snperior delicacy of theocritus i can not discover nor can indeed find that either in the one or the other image there be any want of delicacy which of the two image be less common in the time of the poet who use it for on that consideration the merit of novelty depend i think it be now out of any critick 's power to decide he remark i be afraid with too much justice that there be not a single new think in the pastoral and with equal reason declare that they chief beauty consist in they correct and musical versification which have so influence the english ear as to render every moderate rhymer harmonious in he examination of the messiah he justly observe some deviation from the inspired author which weaken the imagery and dispirit the expression on windsor forest he declare i think without proof that descriptive poetry be by no mean the excellence of pope he draw this inference from the few image introduce in this poem which would not equally belong to any other place he must inquire whether windsor forest have in reality any thing peculiar the stag-chase be not he say so full so animated and so circumstantiated as somerville 's barely to say that one performance be not so good as another be to criticise with little exactness but pope have direct that we should in every work regard the author 's end the stag-chase be the main subject of somerville and might therefore be properly dilate into all its circumstance in pope it be only incidental and be to be despatch in a few line he make a just observation that the description of the external beauty of nature be usually the first effort of a young genius before he have study nature and passion some of milton 's most early as well as mo ' t exquisite piece be he lycida l'allegro and il penseroso if we may except he ode on the nativity of christ which be indeed prior in order of time and in which a penetrate critick might have observe the seed of that boundless imagination which be one day to produce the paradise lose mention thomson and other descriptive poet he remark that writer fail in they copy for want of acquaintance with original and justly ridicule those who think they can form just idea of valley mountain and river in a garret in the strand for this reason i can not regret with this author that pope lay aside he design of write american pastoral for as he must have paint scene which he never see and manners which he never know he performance though it might have be a please amusement of fancy would have exhibit no representation of nature or of life after the pastoral the critick consider the lyrick poetry of pope and dwell longest on the answer we complaint fort be rise upon fort and one invasion make a precedent for another this confidence of the french be exalt by some real advantage if they possess in those country less than we they have more to gain and less to hazard if they be less numerous they be better unite the french compose one body with one head they have all the same interest and agree to pursue it by the same means they be subject to a governor commission by a absolute monarch and participate the authority of he master design be therefore form without debate and execute without impediment they have yet more martial than mercantile ambition and seldom suffer they military scheme to be entangled with collateral project of gain : they have no wish but for conquest of which they justly consider riches as the consequence some advantage they will always have as invader they make war at the hazard of they enemy : the contest be carry on in we territory we must lose more by a victory than they will suffer by a defeat they will subsist while they stay upon we plantation and perhaps destroy they when they can stay no longer if we pursue they and carry the war into they dominion we difficulty will increase every step as we advance for we shall leave plenty behind we and find nothing in canada but lake and forest barren and trackless we enemy will shut themselves up in they fort against which it be difficult to bring cannon through so rough a country and which if they be provide with good magazine will soon starve those who besiege they all these be the natural effect of they government and situation they be accidentally more formidable as they be less happy but the favor of the indian which they enjoy with very few exception among all the nation of the northern continent we ought to consider with other thought this favor we might have enjoy if we have be careful to deserve it the french by have these savage nation on they side be always supply with spy and guide and with auxiliary like the tartar to the turk or the hussar to the german of no great use against troops range in order of battle but very well qualify to maintain a war among wood and rivulet where much mischief may be do by unexpected onset and safety be obtain by quick retreat they can waste a colony by sudden inroad surprise the straggle planter frighten the inhabitant into town hinder the produce only the necessaries of life wealth be not to be acquire but from foreign dominion and by the transportation of the product of one country into another from this necessity thus justly estimate extent from east to west or from the sea to the interior country bear no proportion to they reach along the coast from north to south it be however understand by a kind of tacit compact among the from east to west unlimited and discretional supposing that as the colony increase they may take land as they shall want they the possession of the coast exclude other navigator and the unhappy indian therefore they resolve to remove and to fix at they own discretion the western border of we colony which be heretofore consider as unlimited thus by form a line of fort in some measure parallel to the vigor of they administration they great security be the friendship of the native and to this advantage they have certainly a indubitable right because it be the consequence of they virtue it be ridiculous to imagine that the friendship of nation whether civil or barbarous can be gain and keep but by kind treatment and surely they who intrude uncalled upon the country of a distant people ought to consider the native as worthy of common kindness and content themselves to rob without insulting they the french as have be already observe admit the indian by intermarriage to a equality commander regularity may in time produce a kind of mechanical obedience to signal and command like that which the perverse cartesian impute to animal discipline may impress such a awe upon the mind that any danger shall be less dreaded than the danger of punishment and confidence in the wisdom or fortune of the general may induce the soldier to follow he blindly to the most dangerous enterprise what may be do by discipline and regularity may be see in the troops of the russian emperess and prussian monarch we find that they may be break without confusion and repulse without flight but the english troops have none of these requisite in any eminent degree regularity be by no means part of they character : they be rarely exercise and therefore show very little dexterity in they evolution while he live inoffensively to retain he own opinion of english rights unmolested in he conscience by a oath of abjuration life of eminent person father paul sarpus -lrb- 33 -rrb- father paul whose name before he enter into the monastick life be peter sarpus be bear at venice August 14 1552 he father follow merchandise but with so little success that at he death he leave he family very ill provide for but under the care of a mother whose piety be likely to bring the blessing of providence upon they and whose wise conduct supply the want of fortune by advantage of greater value happily for young sarpus she have a brother master of a celebrated school under whose direction he be place by she here he lose no time but cultivate he ability naturally of the first rate with unwearied application he be bear for study have a natural aversion to pleasure and gaiety and a memory so tenacious that he could repeat thirty verse upon once hear they proportionable to he capacity be he progress in literature : at thirteen have make himself master of school-learning he turn he study to philosophy and the mathematick and enter upon logick under capellum of cremona who though a celebrated master of that science confess himself in a very little time unable to give he pupil further instruction as capellum be of the order of the servite he scholar be induce by he acquaintance with he to engage in the same profession though he uncle and he mother represent to he the hardship and austerity of that kind of life and advise he with great zeal against it but he be steady in he resolution and in 1566 take the habit of the order be then only in he fourteenth year a time of life in most person very improper for such engagement but in he attend with such maturity of thought and such a settle temper that he never seem to regret the choice he then make and which he confirm by a solemn publick profession in 1572 at a general chapter of the servite hold at mantua paul for so we shall now call he be then only twenty year old distinguish himself so much in a publick disputation by he genius and learn that william duke of mantua a great patron of letter solicit the consent of he superior to retain he at he court and not only make he publick professor of divinity in the cathedral but honor he with many proof of he esteem but father paul find a court life not agreeable to he temper quit it two year afterwards and retire to he beloved privacy be then not only acquaint with the latin greek hebrew and chaldee language but with philosophy the mathematick canon and civil law all part of natural philosophy and chymistry itself for he application be unremitted he head clear he apprehension quick and he memory retentive be make a priest at twenty-two he be distinguish by the illustrious cardinal borromeo with he confidence and employ by he on many occasion not without the envy of person of less merit who be so far exasperate as to lay a charge against he before the inquisition for deny that the trinity could be prove from the first chapter of genesis but the accusation be too ridiculous to be take notice of after this he pass successively through the dignity of he order and in the interval of he employment apply himself to he study with so extensive a capacity as leave no branch of knowledge untouched by he acquapendente the great anatomist confess that he be inform how vision be perform and there be proof that he be not a stranger to the circulation of the blood he frequently converse upon astronomy with mathematician upon anatomy with surgeon upon medicine with physician and with chymist upon the analysis of metal not as a superficial inquirer but as a complete master but the hour of repose that he employ so well be interrupt by a new information in the inquisition where a former acquaintance produce a letter write by he in cipher in which he say that he detest the court of rome and that no preferment be obtain there but by dishonest means this accusation however dangerous be pass over on account of he great reputation but make such impression on that court that he be afterward deny a bishoprick by clement the eighth after these difficulty be surmount father paul again retire to he solitude where he appear by some writings draw up by he at that time to have turn he attention more to improvement in piety than learn such be the care with which he read the scripture that it be he custom to draw a line under any passage which he intend more nicely to consider there be not a single word in he new testament but be underline the same mark of attention appear in he old testament psalter and breviary but the most active scene of he life begin about the year 1615 when pope paul the fifth exasperated by some decree of the senate of venice that interfere with the pretended rights of the church lay the whole state under a interdict the senate fill with indignation at this treatment forbid the bishop to receive or publish the pope 's bull and convene the rector of the church command they to celebrate divine service in the accustomed manner with which most of they readily comply but the jesuit and some other refuse be by a solemn edict expel the state both party have proceed to extremity employ they ablest writer to defend they measure : on the pope 's side among other cardinal bellarmine enter the list and with he confederate author defend the papal claim with great scurrility of expression and very sophistical reasoning which be confute by the venetian apologist in much more decent language and with much greater solidity of argument on this occasion father paul be most eminently distinguish by he defense of the rights of the supreme magistrate he treatise of excommunication translate from gerson with a apology and other writings for which he be cite before the inquisition at rome but it may be easily imagine that he do not obey the summons the venetian writer whatever might be the boerhaave be bear on the last day of December 1668 about one in the morning at voorhout a village two mile distant from leyden : he father jame boerhaave be minister of voorhout of whom he son -lrb- any change of he fortune or alteration in he affair whether happy or unfortunate affect he countenance he be always cheerful and desirous of promote mirth by a facetious and humorous conversation he enemy by severity of censure for he never dwell on the fault or defect of other and be so far from inflame the envy of he rival by dwelling on he own excellency that he rarely mention himself or he writings he be not to be overawe or depress by the presence frown or insolence of great man but persist on all occasion in the right with a resolution always present and always calm he be modest but not timorous and firm without rudeness he could with uncommon readiness and certainty make a conjecture of man 's inclination and capacity by they aspect he method of life be to study in the morning and evening and to allot the middle of the day to he publick business he usual exercise be ride till in he latter year he distemper make it more proper for he to walk : when he be weary he amuse himself with play on the violin he greatest pleasure be to retire to he house in the country where he have a garden store with all the herb and tree which the climate would bear here he use to enjoy he hour unmolested and prosecute he study without interruption the diligence with which he pursue he study be sufficiently evident from he success statesman and general may grow great by unexpected accident and a fortunate concurrence of circumstance neither procure nor foresee by themselves but reputation in the learn world must be the effect of industry and capacity boerhaave lose none of he hour but when he have attain one science attempt another he add physick to divinity chymistry to the mathematick and anatomy to botany he examine system by experiment and form experiment into system he neither neglect the observation of other nor blindly submit to celebrated name he neither think so highly of himself as to imagine he could receive no light from book nor so meanly as to believe he could discover nothing but what be to be learn from they he examine the observation of other man but trust only to he own nor be he unacquaint with the art of recommend truth by elegance and embellish the philosopher with polite literature : he know that but a small part of mankind will sacrifice they pleasure to they improvement and those author who would find many reader must endeavor to please while they instruct he know the importance of he own writings to mankind and lest he might by a roughness and barbarity of style too frequent among man of great learning disappoint he own intention and make he labor less useful he do not neglect the politer art of eloquence and poetry thus be he learning at once various and exact profound and agreeable but he knowledge however uncommon hold in he character but the second place he virtue be yet much more uncommon than he learn he be a admirable example of temperance fortitude humility and devotion he piety and a religious sense of he dependance on god be the basis of all he virtue and the principle of he whole conduct he be too sensible of he weakness to ascribe any thing to himself or to conceive that he could subdue passion or withstand temptation by he own natural power he attribute every good thought and every laudable action to the father of goodness be once ask by a friend who have often admire he patience under great provocation whether he know what it be to be angry and by what mean he have so entirely suppress that impetuous and ungovernable passion he answer with the utmost frankness and sincerity that he be naturally quick of resentment but that he have by daily prayer and meditation at length attain to this mastery over himself as soon as he arise in the morning it be throughout he whole life he daily practice to retire for a hour to private prayer and meditation this he often tell he friend give he spirit and vigor in the business of the day and this he therefore commend as the best rule of life for nothing he know could support the soul in all distress but a confidence in the supreme be nor can a steady and rational magnanimity flow from any other source than a consciousness of the divine favor he assert on all occasion the divine authority and sacred efficacy of the holy scripture and maintain that they alone teach the way of salvation and that they only could give peace of mind the excellency of the christian religion be the frequent subject of he conversation a strict obedience to the doctrine and a diligent imitation of the example of we bless savior he often declare to be the foundation of true tranquillity he recommend to he friend a careful observation of the precept of mose concern the love of god and man he worship god as he be in himself without attempt to inquire into he nature he desire only to think of god what god know of himself there he stop lest by indulge he own idea he should form a deity from he own imagination and sin by fall down before he to the will of god he pay a absolute submission without endeavor to discover the reason of he determination and this he account the first and most inviolable duty of a christian when he hear of a criminal condemn to die he use to think : who can tell whether this man be not better than i or if i be better it be not to be ascribe to myself but to the goodness of god such be the sentiment of boerhaave whose word we have add in the note -lrb- 39 -rrb- so far be this man from be make impious by philosophy or vain by knowledge or by virtue that he ascribe all he ability to the bounty and all he goodness to the grace of god may he example extend its influence to he admirer and follower may those who study he writings imitate he life and those who endeavor after he knowledge aspire likewise to he piety he marry September 17 1710 mary drolenveaux the only daughter of a burgomaster of leyden by whom he have joanna marium who survive she father and three other child who die in they infancy the he achievement and those of we present admiral robert blake be bear at bridgewater in somersetshire in August 1598 he father be a merchant of that place who have acquire a considerable fortune by the name and he memory nor have any writer dare to deny he the praise of intrepidity honesty contempt of wealth and love of he country he be the first man say clarendon that decline the old track and make to make a noise only and to fright those who could rarely be hurt by they he be the first that infuse that proportion of courage into seaman by make they see by experience what mighty thing they could bold and resolute achievement to this attestation of he military excellence it may be proper to subjoin a account of he moral character from the author of life english and foreign he be jealous say that writer of the liberty of the subject and the glory of he nation and as he make use of no mean artifice to raise himself to the highest command at sea so he need no interest but he merit to support he in it he scorn nothing more than money which as fast as it come in be lay out by he in the service of the state and to show that he be animated by that brave publick spirit which have since with one incident of he life by which it appear how much the spirit of blake be superior to all private view he brother in the last action with the spaniard have not do he duty be at blake 's desire discard and the ship be give to another yet be he not less regardful of he as a brother for when he die he leave he he estate know he well qualify to adorn or enjoy a private fortune though he have qu mr well of bridgewater -- ed sir franci drake -lrb- 44 -rrb- franci drake be the son of a clergyman in devonshire who be incline to the doctrine of the protestant at that time much oppose by henry the eighth be oblige to fly from he place of residence into kent for refuge from the persecution raise against he and those of the same opinion by the law of the six article how long he live there or how he be support be not know nor have we any account of the first year of sir franci drake 's life of any disposition to hazard and adventure which might have be discover in he childhood or of the education which qualify he for such wonderful attempt we be only inform that he be put apprentice by he father to the master of a small vessel that trade to france and the low country under whom he probably learn the rudiment of navigation and familiarize himself to the danger and hardship of the sea but how few opportunity soever he might have in this part of he life for the exercise of he courage he give so many proof of diligence and fidelity that he master die unmarried leave he he little vessel in reward of he service a circumstance that deserve to be remember not only as it may illustrate the private character of this brave man but as it may hint to all those who may hereafter propose he conduct for they imitation that virtue be the surest foundation both of reputation and fortune and that the first step to greatness be to be honest if it be not improper to dwell longer on a incident at the first view so inconsiderable it might be add that it deserve the reflection of those who when they be engage in affair not adequate to they ability pass they over with a contemptuous neglect and while they amuse themselves with chimerical scheme and plan of future undertaking suffer every opportunity of smaller advantage to slip away as unworthy they regard they may learn from the example of drake that diligence in employment of less consequence be the most successful introduction to greater enterprise after have follow for some time he master 's profession he grow weary of so narrow a province and have sell he little vessel venture he effect in the new trade to the west indy which have not be long discover and very little frequented by the english till that time be conceive so much to abound in wealth that no voyage thither could fail of be recompense by great advantage nothing be talk of among the mercantile or adventurous part of mankind but the beauty and riches of the new world fresh discovery be frequently make new country and nation never hear of before be daily describe and it may easily be conclude that the relater do not diminish the merit of they attempt by suppress or diminish any circumstance that might produce wonder or excite curiosity nor be they vanity only engage in raise admirer but they interest likewise in procure adventurer who be indeed easily gain by the hope which naturally arise from new prospects though through ignorance of the american sea and by the malice of the spaniard who from the first discovery of those country consider every other nation that attempt to follow they as invader of they rights the best concerted design often miscarry among those who suffer most from the spanish injustice be captain john hawkin who have be admit by the viceroy to traffick in the bay of mexico be contrary to the stipulation then make between they and in violation of the peace between spain and england attack without any declaration of hostility and oblige after a obstinate resistance to retire with the loss of four ship and a great number of he man who be either destroy or carry into slavery in this voyage drake have adventure almost all he fortune which he in vain endeavor to recover both by he own private interest and by obtain letter from queen elizabeth for the spaniard deaf to all remonstrance either vindicate the injustice of the viceroy or at least forbore to redress it drake thus oppressed and impoverished retain at least he courage and he industry that ardent spirit that prompt he to adventure and that indefatigable patience that enable he to surmount difficulty he do not sit down idly to lament misfortune which heaven have put it in he power to remedy or to repine at poverty while the wealth of he enemy be to be gain but have make two voyage to america for the sake of gain intelligence of the state of the spanish settlement and acquaint himself with the sea and coast he determine on a third expedition of more importance by which the spaniard should find how imprudently they always act who injure and insult a brave man on the 24th of may 1572 franci drake set sail from plymouth in the pascha of seventy ton accompany by the swan of twenty-five ton command by he brother john drake have in both the vessel seventy-three man and boy with a year 's provision and such artillery and ammunition as be necessary for he undertaking which however incredible it may appear to such as consider rather he force than he fortitude be no less than to make reprisal upon the most powerful nation in the world the wind continue favourable they enter June 29th between guadaloupe and dominica and on July 6th see the highland of santa martha then continue they course after have be becalm for some time they arrive at port pheasant so name by drake in a former voyage to the east of nombre de dios here he propose to build he pinnace which he have bring in piece ready frame from plymouth and be go ashore with a few man unarmed but discover a smoke at a distance order the other boat to follow he with a greater force then march towards the fire which be in the top of a high tree he find a plate of lead nail to another tree with a inscription engrave upon it by one garret a englishman who have leave that place but five day before and have take this method of inform he that the spaniard have be advertise of he intention to anchor at that place and that it therefore would be prudent to make a very short stay there but drake know how convenient this place be for he design and consider that the hazard and waste of time which could not be avoid in seek another station be equivalent to any other danger which be to be apprehend from the spaniard determine to follow he first resolution only for he greater security he order a kind of palisade or fortification to be make by fell large tree and lay the trunk and branch one upon another by the side of the river on July 20th have build they pinnace and be join by one captain rause who happen to touch at the same place with a bark of fifty man they set sail towards nombre de dios and take two frigate at the island of pine be inform by the negro which they find in they that the inhabitant of that place be in expectation of some soldier which the governor of panama have promise to defend they from the symeron or fugitive negro who have escape from the tyranny of they master in great number have settle themselves under two king or leader on each side of the way between nombre de dios and panama and not only assert they natural right to liberty and independence but endeavor to revenge the cruelty they have suffer and have lately put the inhabitant of nombre de dios into the utmost consternation these negro the captain set on shore on the mainland so that they might by join the symeron recover they liberty or at least might not have it in they power to give the people of nombre de dios any speedy information of he intention to invade they then select fifty-three man from he own company and twenty from the crew of he new associate captain rause he embark with they in he pinnace and set sail for nombre de dios on July the 28th at night he approach the town undiscovered and dropt he anchor under the shore intend after he man be refresh to begin the attack but find that they be terrifying each other with formidable account of the strength of the place and the multitude of the inhabitant he determine to hinder the panick from spread further by lead they immediately to action and therefore order they to they par he land without any opposition there be only one gunner upon the bay though it be secure with six brass cannon of the largest size ready mount but the gunner while they be throw the cannon from they carriage alarm the town as they soon discover by the bell the drum and the noise of the people drake leave twelve man to guard the pinnace march round the town with no great opposition the man be more hurt by tread on the weapon leave on the ground by the fly enemy than by the resistance which they encounter at length have take some of the spaniard drake command they to show he the governor 's house where the mule that bring the silver from panama be unloaded there they find the door open and enter the room where the silver be reposited find it heap up in bar in such quantity as almost exceed belief the pile be they conjecture seventy foot in length ten in breadth and twelve in height each bar weigh between thirty and forty-five pound it be easy to imagine that at the sight of this treasure nothing be think on by the english but by what mean they might best convey it to they boat and doubtless it be not easy for drake who consider they distance from the shore and the number of they enemy be afraid of be intercept in he retreat to hinder he man from encumber themselves with so much silver as might have retard they march and obstruct the use of they weapon however by promise to lead they to the king 's treasurehouse where there and see if the same terror have take possession of the man whom he have leave to guard he boat but find no foundation for these dreadful apprehension he persist in he first design and lead the troop forward to the treasurehouse in they way there fall a violent shower of rain which wet some of they bowstring and extinguish many of they match a misfortune which might soon have be repair and which perhaps the enemy might suffer in common with they but which however on this occasion very much embarrassed they as the delay produce by it repress that ardor which sometimes be only to be keep up by continue action and give time to the timorous and slothful to spread they insinuation and propagate they cowardice some whose fear be they predominant passion be continually magnify the number and plenty and security and with the most probable expectation of immense riches on the 16th of October he anchor within sight of carthagena without landing and on the 17th go out to sea take a spanish know drake to lie at the mouth of the harbor send out they vessel on purpose to be take do not appear perhaps they think that in order to keep possession of he prize he would divide he company and by that division be more easily destroy in a few hour afterwards they send out two frigate well man which drake soon force to retire and have sink one of he prize and burn the other in they sight leap enemy 's coast in sight of a superior force only to show how little they be fear be a act that would in these time meet with little applause nor can the general be seriously commend or rationally vindicate who expose he person to destruction and by consequence he expedition to miscarriage only for the pleasure of a idle insult a insignificant bravado all that can be urge in he defense be that perhaps it might contribute to heighten the esteem of he follower as few man especially of that class be philosophical enough to state the exact limit of prudence and bravery or not to be dazzle with a intrepidity how improperly soever exert it may be add that perhaps the spaniard whose notion of courage be sufficiently romantick might look upon he as a more formidable enemy and yield more easily
