valuable part away and would have permit they no less peaceably to burn they ship but drake never make war with a spirit of cruelty or revenge or carry hostility further than be necessary for he own advantage or defense they set sail the next morning towards panama in quest of the caca fuego a very rich ship which have sail fourteen day before bind thither from lima which they overtake on the 1st of march near cape francisco and board it find not only a quantity of jewel and twelve chest of ryal of plate but eighty pound weight of gold and twenty-six ton of uncoined silver with piece of work plate to a great value in unlade this prize they spend six day and then dismiss the spaniard stand off to sea be now sufficiently enriched and have lose all hope of find they associate and perhaps begin to be infect with that desire of ease and pleasure which be the natural consequence of wealth obtain by danger and fatigue they begin to consult about they return home and in pursuance of drake 's advice resolve first to find out some convenient harbor where they might supply themselves with wood and water and then endeavor to discover a passage from the south sea into the atlantick ocean a discovery which would not only enable they to return home with less danger and in a shorter time but would much facilitate the navigation in those part of the world for this purpose they have recourse to a port in the island of caine where they meet with fish wood and fresh water and in they course take a ship laden with silk and linen which be the last that they meet with on the coast of america but be desirous of store themselves for a long course they touch April the 15th at guatulco a spanish island where they supply themselves with provision and seize a bushel of ryal of silver from guatulco which lie in 15 deg 40 min they stand out to sea and without approach any land sail forward till on the night follow the 3rd of June be then in the latitude of thirty-eight degree they be suddenly benumb with such cold blast that they be scarcely able to handle the rope this cold increase upon they as they proceed to such a degree that the sailor be discourage from mount upon the deck nor be the effect of the climate to be imputed to the warmth of the region to which they have be lately accustom for the rope be stiff with frost and the meat could scarcely be convey warm to the table on June 17th they come to anchor in 38 deg 30 min when they see the land naked and the tree without leaf and in a short time have opportunity of observe that the native of that country be not less sensible of the cold than themselves for the next day come a man rowing in he canoe towards the ship and at a distance from it make a long oration with very extraordinary gesticulation and great appearance of vehemence and a little time afterwards make a second visit in the same manner and then return a third time he present they after he harangue be finish with a kind of crown of black feather such as they king wear upon they head and a basket of rush fill with a particular herb both which he fasten to a short stick and throw into the boat nor could he be prevail upon to receive any thing in return though push towards he upon a board only he take up a hat which be fling into the water three day afterwards they ship have receive some damage at sea be bring nearer to land that the lading might be take out in order to which the english who have now learn not too negligently to commit they life to the mercy of savage nation raise a kind of fortification with stone and build they tent within it all this be not behold by the inhabitant without the utmost astonishment which incite they to come down in crowd to the coast with no other view as it appear than to worship the new divinity that have condescend to touch upon they country drake be far from countenance they error or take advantage of they weakness to injure or molest they and therefore have direct they to lay aside they bow and arrow he present they with linen and other necessaries of which he show they the use they then return to they habitation about three quarter of a mile from the english camp where they make such loud and violent outcry that they be hear by the english who find that they still persist in they first notion and be pay they they kind of melancholy adoration two day afterwards they perceive the approach of a far more numerous company who stop at the top of a hill which overlook the english settlement while one of they make a long oration at the end of which all the assembly bow they body and pronounce the syllable _ oh _ with a solemn tone as by way of confirmation of what have be say by the orator then the man lay down they bow and leave the woman and child on the top of the hill come down towards the tent and seem transport in the highest degree at the kindness of the general who receive they gift and admit they to he presence the woman at a distance appear seize with a kind of phrensy such as that of old among the pagan in some of they religious ceremony and in honor as it seem of they guest tear they cheek and bosom with they nail and throw themselves upon the stone with they naked body till they be cover with blood these cruel rite and mistaken honor be by no means agreeable to drake whose predominant sentiment be notion of piety and therefore not to make that criminal in himself by he concurrence which perhaps ignorance might make guiltless in they he order he whole company to fall upon they knee and with they eye lift up to heaven that the savage might observe that they worship be address to a be reside there they all join in pray that this harmless and deluded people might be bring to the knowledge of the true religion and the doctrine of we bless savior after which they sing psalm a performance so please to they wild audience that in all they visit they generally first accost they with a request that they would sing they then return all the present which they have receive and retire three day after this on June 25 1579 we general receive two ambassador from the hioh or king of the country who intend to visit the camp require that some token might be send he of friendship and peace this request be readily comply with and soon after come the king attend by a guard of about a hundred tall man and precede by a officer of state who carry a sceptre make of black wood adorn with chain of a kind of bone or horn which be mark of the highest honor among they and have two crown make as before with feather fasten to it with a bag of the same herb which be present to drake at he first arrival behind he be the king himself dress in a coat of cony-skin with a caul weave with feather upon he head a ornament so much in estimation there that none but the domestick of the king be allow to wear it he attendant follow he adorn nearly in the same manner and after they come the common people with basket plait so artificially that they hold water in which by way of sacrifice they bring root and fish drake not lull into security range he man in order of battle and wait they approach who come nearer stand still while the sceptre-bearer make a oration at the conclusion of which they again come forward to the foot of the hill and then the sceptre-bearer begin a song which he accompany with a dance in both which the man join but the woman dance without singing drake now distrust they no longer admit they into he fortification where they continue they song and dance a short time and then both the king and some other of the company make long harangue in which it appear by the rest of they behavior that they entreat he to accept of they country and to take the government of it into he own hand for the king with the apparent concurrence of the rest place the crown upon he head grace he with the chain and other sign of authority and salute he with the title of hioh the kingdom thus offer though of no further value to he than as it furnish he with present necessaries drake think it not prudent to refuse and therefore take possession of it in the name of queen elizabeth not without ardent wish that this acquisition might have be of use to he native country and that so mild and innocent a people might have be unite to the church of christ the kingdom be thus consign and the grand affair at a end the common people leave they king and he domestick with drake and disperse themselves over the camp and when they see any one that please they by he appearance more than the rest they tear they flesh and vent they outcry as before in token of reverence and admiration they then proceed to show they they wound and disease in hope of a miraculous and instantaneous cure to which the english to benefit and undeceive they at the same time apply such remedy as they use on the like occasion they be now grow confident and familiar and come down to the camp every day repeat they ceremony and sacrifice till they be more fully inform how disagreeable they be to those whose favor they be so studious of obtain : they then visit they without adoration indeed but with a curiosity so ardent that it leave they no leisure to provide the necessaries of life with which the english be therefore oblige to supply they they have then sufficient opportunity to remark the custom and disposition of these new ally whom they find tractable and benevolent strong of body far beyond the english yet unfurnished with weapon either for assault or defense they bow be too weak for any thing but sport they dexterity in take fish be such that if they see they so near the shore that they could come to they without swim they never miss they the same curiosity that have bring they in such crowd to the shore now induce drake and some of he company to travel up into the country which they find at some distance from the coast very fruitful fill with large deer and abound with a peculiar kind of cony smaller than ours with tail like that of a rat and paw such as those of a mole they have bag under they chin in which they carry provision to they young the house of the inhabitant be round hole dig in the ground from the brink of which they raise rafter or pile shelve towards the middle where they all meet and be cram together they lie upon rush with the fire in the midst and let the smoke fly out at the door the man be generally naked but the woman make a kind of petticoat of bulrush which they comb like hemp and throw the skin of a deer over they shoulder they be very modest tractable and obedient to they husband such be the condition of this people and not very different be perhaps the state of the greatest part of mankind whether more enlighten nation ought to look upon they with pity as less happy than themselves some skeptick have make very unnecessarily a difficulty of determine more they say be lose by the perplexity than gain by the instruction of science we enlarge we vice with we knowledge and multiply we want with we attainment and the happiness of life be better secure by the ignorance of vice than by the knowledge of virtue the fallacy by which such reasoner have impose upon themselves seem to arise from the comparison which they make not between two man equally incline to apply the means of happiness in they power to the end for which providence confer they but furnish in unequal proportion with the means of happiness which be the true state of savage and polished nation but between two man of which he to whom providence have be most bountiful destroy the blessing by negligence or obstinate misuse while the other steady diligent and virtuous employ he ability and convenience to they proper end the question be not whether a good indian or bad englishman be most happy but which state be most desirable suppose virtue and reason the same in both nor be this the only mistake which be generally admit in this controversy for these reasoner frequently confound innocence with the mere incapacity of guilt he that never see or hear or think of strong liquor can not be propose as a pattern of sobriety this land be name by drake albion from its white cliff in which it bear some resemblance to he native country and the whole history of the resignation of it to the english be engraven on a piece of brass then nail on a post and fix up before they departure which be now discover by the people to be near at hand they could not forbear perpetual lamentation when the english on the 23rd of July weigh anchor they see they climb to the top of hill that they might keep they in sight and observe fire light up in many part of the country on which as they suppose sacrifice be offer near this harbor they touch at some island where they find great number of seal and despair now to find any passage through the northern part he after a general consultation determine to steer away to the molucca and set sail July 25th he sail for sixty-eight day without sight of land and on September 30th arrive within view of some island situate about eight degree northward from the line from whence the inhabitant resort to they in canoe hollow out of the solid trunk of a tree and raise at both end so high above the water that they seem almost a semicircle they be burnish in such a manner that they shine like ebony and be keep steady by a piece of timber fix on each side of they with strong cane that be fasten at one end to the boat and at the other to the end of the timber the first company that come bring fruit potato and other thing of no great value with a appearance of traffick and exchange they lading for other commodity with great show of honesty and friendship but have as they imagine lay all suspicion asleep they soon send another fleet of canoe of which the crew behave with all the insolence of tyrant and all the rapacity of thief for whatever be suffer to come into they hand they seem to consider as they own and would neither pay for it nor restore it and at length find the english resolve to admit they no longer they discharge a shower of stone from they boat which insult drake prudently and generously return by order a piece of ordnance to be fire without hurt they at which they be so terrify that they leap into the water and hide themselves under the canoe have for some time but little wind they do not arrive at the molucca till the 3rd of November and then design to touch at tidore they be visit as they sail by a little island belong to the king of ternate by the viceroy of the place who inform they that it would be more advantageous for they to have recourse to he master for supplies and assistance than to the king of ternate who be in some degree dependent on the portuguese and that he would himself carry the news of they arrival and prepare for they reception drake be by the argument of the viceroy prevail upon to alter he resolution and on November 5 cast anchor before ternate and scarce be he arrive before the viceroy with other of the chief noble come out in three large boat row by forty man on each side to conduct the ship into a safe harbor and soon after the king himself have receive a velvet cloak by a messenger from drake as a token of peace come with such a retinue and dignity of appearance as be not expect in those remote part of the world he be receive with discharge of cannon and every kind of musick with which he be so much delighted that desire the musician to come down into the boat he be tow along in it at the stern of the ship the king be of a graceful stature and regal carriage of a mild aspect and low voice he attendant be dress in white cotton or calico of whom some whose age give they a venerable appearance seem he counsellor and the rest officer or noble he guard be not ignorant of firearm but have not many among they be equip for the most part with bow and dart the king have spend some time in admire the multitude of new object that present themselves retire as soon as the ship be bring to anchor and promise to return on the day follow and in the mean time the inhabitant have leave to traffick bring down provision in great abundance at the time when the king be expect he brother come on board to request of drake that he would come to the castle propose to stay himself as a hostage for he return drake refuse to go but direct to walk : there they find threescore old man privy counsellor to the king and on each side of the door without stand four old man of foreign country who serve as interpreter in commerce in a value over he head be bear a rich canopy and by he chair of state on which he sit down when he have enter the house stand a page with a fan set with sapphire to moderate the excess of the heat here he receive the compliment of the english and then honourably dismiss they the castle which they have some opportunity of observe seem of no great force it be build by the portuguese who attempt to reduce this kingdom into a absolute subjection murder the king and intend to pursue they scheme by the destruction of all he son but the general abhorrence which cruelty and perfidy naturally excite arm all the nation against they and procure they total expulsion from all the dominion of ternate which from that time increase in power continue to make new conquest and to deprive they of other acquisition while they lay before ternate a gentleman come on board attend by he interpreter he be dress somewhat in the european manner and soon distinguish himself from the native of ternate or any other he repair he vessel and refresh he man without danger or interruption leave this place the 12th of December they sail towards the celebe but have a wind not very favourable they be detain among a the rock on which they have strike rise perpendicularly from the water and that there be no anchorage nor any bottom to be find a boat 's length from the ship but this discovery with its consequence be by drake wisely conceal from the common sailor lest they should abandon themselves to despair for which there be indeed cause there be no prospect leave but that they must there sink with the ship which must undoubtedly be soon dash to piece or perish in attempt to reach the shore in they boat or be cut in piece by barbarian if they should arrive at land in the midst of this perplexity and distress drake direct that the sacrament should be administer and he man fortify with all the consolation which religion afford then persuade they to lighten the vessel by throw into the sea part of they lading which be cheerfully comply with but without effect at length when they hope have forsake they and no new struggle could be make they be on a sudden relieve by a remission of the wind which have hitherto blow strongly against the side of the ship which lay towards the sea hold it upright against the rock but when the blast slacken be then low water the ship lie higher with that part which rest on the rock than with the other and be bear up no longer by the wind reel into the deep water to the surprise and joy of drake and he companion this be the greatest and most inextricable distress which they have ever suffer and make such a impression upon they mind that for some time afterwards they dare not adventure to spread they sail but go slowly forward with the utmost circumspection they thus continue they course without any observable occurrence till on the 11th of march they come to a anchor before the island of java and send to the king a present of cloth and silk receive from he in return a large quantity of provision and the day follow drake go himself on shore and entertain the king with he musick and obtain leave to store he ship with provision the island be govern by a great number of petty king or raia subordinate to one chief of these prince three come on board together a few day after they arrival and have upon they return recount the wonder warlike armament and instrument of navigation this intercourse of civility somewhat retard the business for which they come but at length they not only victual they ship but cleanse the bottom which method produce more frequently a temporary suspension of dispute than a final termination court of justice be therefore necessary with a settle method of procedure of which the most simple be to cite the party to hear they plea and dismiss they with immediate decision this however be in many case impracticable and in other be so seldom practice that it be frequent rather to incur loss than to seek then if not before commence tutor and publickly undertake the education of young man a tutor of one-and-tweuty however accomplish with learn however exalt by genius would now gain little reverence or obedience but in those day of discipline and regularity the authority of the statute easily supply that of the teacher all power that be lawful be reverenced besides young tutor have still younger pupil ascham be say to have court he scholar to study by every incitement to have treat they with great kindness and to have take care at once to instil learning and piety to enlighten they mind and to form they manners many of he scholar rise to great eminence and among they william grindal be so much distinguish that by cheke 's recommendation he be call to court as a proper master of language for the lady elizabeth there be yet no established lecturer of greek the university therefore appoint ascham to read in the open school and pay he out of the publick purse a honorary stipend such as be then reckon sufficiently liberal a lecture be afterwards found by king henry and he then quit the school but continue to explain greek author in he own college he be at first a opponent of the new pronunciation introduce or rather of the ancient restore about this time by cheke and smith and make some cautious struggle for the common practice which the credit and dignity of he antagonist do not permit he to defend very publickly or with much vehemence : nor be they long he antagonist for either he affection for they merit or he conviction of the cogency of they argument soon change he opinion and he practice and he adhere ever after to they method of utterance of this controversy it be not necessary to give a circumstantial account something of it may be find in strype 's life of smith and something in baker 's reflection upon learn it be sufficient to remark here that cheke 's pronunciation be that which now prevail in the school of england disquisition not only verbal but merely literal be too minute for popular narration he be not less eminent as a writer of latin than as a teacher of greek all the publick letter of the university be of he composition and as little qualification must often bring great ability into notice he be recommend to this honorable employment not less by the neatness of he hand than the elegance of he style however great be he learning he be not always immure in he chamber but be valetudinary and weak of body think it necessary to spend many hour in such exercise as might best relieve he after the fatigue of study he favorite amusement be archery in which he spend or in the opinion of other lose so much time that those whom either he fault or virtue make he enemy and perhaps some whose kindness wish he always worthily employ do not scruple to censure he practice as unsuitable to a man profess learning and perhaps of bad example in a place of education to free himself from this censure be one of the reason for which he publish in 1544 he toxophilus or the schole or partition of shooting in which he join the praise with the precept of archery he design not only to teach the art of shooting but to give a example of diction more natural and more truly english than be use by the common writer of that age whom he censure for mingle exotick term with they native language and of whom he complain that they be make author not by skill or education but by arrogance and temerity he have not fail in either of he purpose he have sufficiently vindicate archery as a innocent salutary useful and liberal diversion and if he precept be of no great use he have only show by one example among many how little the hand can derive from the mind how little intelligence can conduce to dexterity in every art practice be much in art manual practice be almost the whole : precept can at most but warn against error it can never bestow excellence the bow have be so long disused that most english reader have forget its importance though it be the weapon by which we gain the battle of agincourt a weapon which when handle by english yeoman no foreign troops be able to resist we be not only abler of body than the french and therefore superior in the use of arm which be forcible only in proportion to the strength with which they be handle but the national practice of shooting for pleasure or for prize by which every man be inure to archery from he infancy give we insuperable advantage the bow require more practice to skilful use than any other instrument of offence firearm be then in they infancy and though battering-piece have be some time in use i know not whether any soldier be arm with hand-gun when the toxophilus be first publish they be soon after use by the spanish troops whom other nation make haste to imitate but how little they could yet effect will be understand from the account give by the ingenious author of the exercise for the norfolk militia the first musket be very heavy and could not be fire without a rest they have matchlock and barrel of a wide bear that carry a large ball and charge of powder and do execution at a greater distance the musketeer on a march carry only they rest and ammunition and have boy to bear they musket after they for which they be allow great additional pay they be very slow in load not only by reason of the unwieldiness of the piece and because they carry the powder and ball separate but from the time it take to prepare and adjust the match so that they fire be not near so brisk as ours be now afterwards a lighter kind of matchlock musket come into use and they carry they ammunition in bandelier which be broad belt that come over the shoulder to which be hung several little case of wood cover with leather each contain a charge of powder the ball they carry loose in a pouch and they have also a priming-horn hanging by they side the old english writer call those large musket caliver the harquebuss be a lighter piece that could be fire without a rest the matchlock be fire by a match fix by a kind of tong in the serpentine or cock which by pull the trigger be bring down with great quickness upon the priming in the pan over which there be a slide cover which be draw back by the hand just at the time of firing there be a great deal of nicety and care require to fit the match properly to the cock so as to come down exactly true on the priming to blow the ash from the coal and to guard the pan from the spark that fall from it a great deal of time be also lose in take it out of the cock and return it between the finger of the left hand every time that the piece be fire and wet weather often render the match useless while this be the state of firearm and this state continue among we to the civil war with very little improvement it be no wonder that the long-bow be prefer by sir thoma smith who write of the choice of weapon in the reign of queen elizabeth when the use of the bow still continue though the musket be gradually prevail sir john haward a writer yet later have in he history of the norman king endeavor to evince the superiority of the archer to the musketeer : however in the long peace of king jame the bow be wholly forget gun have from that time be the weapon of the english as of other nation and as they be now improve be certainly more efficacious ascham have yet another reason if not for write he book at least for present it to king henry england be not then what it may be now justly term the capital of literature and therefore those who aspire to superior degree of excellence think it necessary to travel into other country the purse of ascham be not equal to the expense of peregrination and therefore he hope to have it augment by a pension nor be he wholly disappoint for the king reward he with a yearly payment of ten pound a pension of ten pound grant by a king of england to a man of letter appear to modern reader so contemptible a benefaction that it be not unworthy of inquiry what might be its value at that time and how much ascham might be enrich by it nothing be more uncertain than the estimation of wealth by denominate money the precious metal never retain long the same proportion to real commodity and the same name in different age do not imply the same quantity of metal so that it be equally difficult to know how much money be contain in any nominal sum and to find what any suppose quantity of gold or silver would purchase both which be necessary to the commensuration of money or the adjustment of proportion between the same sum at different period of time a numeral pound in king henry 's time contain as now twenty shilling and therefore it must be inquire what twenty shilling could perform bread-corn be the most certain standard of the necessaries of life wheat be generally sell at that time for one shill the bushel if therefore we take five shilling the bushel for the current price ten pound be equivalent to fifty but here be danger of a fallacy it may be doubt whether wheat be the general bread-corn of that age and if rye barley or oats be the common food and wheat as i suspect only a delicacy the value of wheat will not regulate the price of other thing this doubt however be in favor of ascham for if we raise the worth of wheat we raise that of he pension but the value of money have another variation which we be still less able to ascertain : the rule of custom or the different need of artificial life make that revenue little at one time which be great at another man be rich and poor not only in proportion to what they have but to what they want in some age not only necessaries be cheaper but fewer thing be necessary in the age of ascham most of the elegancy and expense of we present fashion be unknown : commerce have not yet distribute superfluity through the lower class of the people and the character of a student imply frugality and require no splendor to support it he pension therefore reckon together the want which he could supply and the want from which he be exempt may be estimate in my opinion at more than one hundred pound a year which add to the income of he fellowship put he far enough above distress this be a year of good fortune to ascham he be choose orator to the university on the removal of sir john cheke to court where he be make tutor to prince edward a man once distinguish soon gain admirer ascham be now receive to notice by many of the nobility and by great lady among whom it be then the fashion to study the ancient language lee archbishop of york allow he a yearly pension how much we be not tell he be probably about this time employ in teach many illustrious person to write a fine hand and among other henry and charle duke of suffolk the princess elizabeth and prince edward henry the eighth die two year after and a reformation of religion be now openly prosecute by king edward and he council ascham who be know to favor it have a new grant of he pension and continue at cambridge where he live in great familiarity with bucer who have be call from germany to the professorship of divinity but he retirement be soon at a end for in 1548 he pupil grindal the master of the princess elizabeth die and the princess who have already some acquaintance with ascham call he from he college to direct she study he obey the summons as we may easily believe with readiness and for two year instruct she with great diligence but then be disgusted either at she or she domestick perhaps eager for another change of life he leave she without she consent and return to the university of this precipitation he long repent and as those who be not accustom to disrespect can not easily forgive it he probably feel the effect of he imprudence to he death after have visit cambridge he take a journey into yorkshire to see he native place and he old acquaintance and there receive a letter from the court inform he that he be appoint secretary to sir richard morisine who be to be despatch as ambassador into germany in he return to london he pay that memorable visit to lady jane gray in which he find she reading the phasdo in greek as he have relate in he schoolmaster in September 1550 he attend morisine to germany and wander over great part of the country make observation upon all that appear worthy of he curiosity and contracting acquaintance with man of learn to he correspondent sturmius he pay a visit but sturmius be not at home and those two illustrious friend never see each other during the course of this embassy ascham undertake to improve morisine in greek and for four day in the week explain some passage in herodotus every morning and more than two hundred verse of sophocle or euripide every afternoon he read with he likewise some of the oration of demosthene on the other day he compile the letter of business and in the night fill up he diary digest he remark and write private letter to he friend in england and particularly to those of he college whom he continually exhort to perseverance in study amidst all the pleasure of novelty which he travels supply and in the dignity of he publick station he prefer the tranquillity of private study and the quiet of academical retirement the reasonableness of this choice have be always dispute and in the contrariety of human interest and disposition the controversy will not easily be decide he make a short excursion into italy and mention in he schoolmaster with great severity the vice of venice he be desirous of visit trent while the council be sit but the scantiness of he purse defeat he curiosity in this journey he write he report and discourse of the affair in germany in which he describe the disposition and interest of the german prince like a man inquisitive and judicious and recount many particularity which be lose in the mass of general history in a style which to the ear of that age be undoubtedly mellifluous and which be now a very valuable specimen of genuine english by the death of king edward in 1553 the reformation be stop morisine be recall and ascham 's pension and hope be at a end he therefore retire to he fellowship in a state of disappointment and despair which he biographer have endeavor to express in the deepest strain of plaintive declamation he be deprive of all he support say graunt strip of he pension and cut off from the assistance of he friend who have now lose they influence : so that he have nec premia nec praedium neither pension nor estate to support he at cambridge there be no credit due to a rhetorician 's account either of good or evil the truth be that ascham still have in he fellowship all that in the early part of he life have give he plenty and might have live like the other inhabitant of the college with the advantage of more knowledge and higher reputation but notwithstanding he love of academical retirement he have now too long enjoy the pleasure and festivity of publick life to return with a good will to academical poverty he have however better fortune than he expect and if he lament he condition like he historian better than he deserve he have during he absence in germany be appoint latin secretary to king edward and by the interest of gardiner bishop of winchester he be instate in the same office under philip and mary with a salary of twenty pound a year soon after he admission to he new employment he give a extraordinary specimen of he ability and diligence by compose and transcribe with he usual elegance in three day forty-seven letter to prince and personage of whom cardinal be the lowest how ascham who be know to be a protestant could preserve the favor of gardiner and hold a place of honor and profit in queen mary 's court it must be very natural to inquire cheke as be well know be compel to a recantation and why ascham be spare can not now be discover graunt at a time when the transaction of queen mary 's reign must have be well enough remembered declare that ascham always make open profession of the reform religion and that englesfield and other often endeavor to incite gardiner against he but find they accusation reject with contempt : yet he allow that suspicion and charge of temporization and compliance have somewhat sullied he reputation the author of the biographium britannica conjecture that he owe he safety to he innocence and usefulness that it would have be unpopular to attack a man so little liable to censure and that the loss of he pen could not have be easily supply but the truth be that morality be never suffer in the day of persecution to protect heresy : nor be we sure that ascham be more clear from common failing than those who suffer more and whatever might be he ability they be not so necessary but gardiner could have easily fill he place with another secretary nothing be more vain than at a distant time to examine the motive of discrimination and partiality for the inquirer have consider interest and policy be oblige at last to admit more frequent and more active motive of human conduct caprice accident and private affection at that time if some be punish many be forborne and of many why should not ascham happen to be one he seem to have be calm and prudent and content with that peace which he be suffer to enjoy : a mode of behavior that seldom fail to produce security he have be abroad in the last year of king edward and have at least give no recent offence he be certainly accord to he own opinion not much in danger for in the next year he resign he fellowship which by gardiner 's favor he have continue to hold though not resident and married margaret howe a young gentle-woman of a good family he be distinguish in this reign by the notice of cardinal pole a man of great candor learning and gentleness of manners and particularly eminent for he skill in latin who think highly of ascham 's style of which it be no inconsiderable proof that when pole be desirous of communicate a speech make by himself as legate in parliament to the pope he employ ascham to translate it he be say to have be not only protect by the officer of state but favor and countenance by the queen herself so that he have no reason of complaint in that reign of turbulence and persecution : nor be he fortune much mend when in 1558 he pupil elizabeth mount the throne he be continue in he former employment with the same stipend but though he be daily admit to the presence of the queen assist she private study and partook of she diversion sometimes read to she in the learn language and sometimes play with she at draught and chess he add nothing to he twenty pound a year but the prebend of westwang in the church of york which be give he the year follow he fortune be therefore not proportionate to the rank which he office and reputation give he or to the favor in which he seem to stand with he mistress of this parsimonious allotment it be again a hopeless search to inquire the reason the queen be not naturally bountiful and perhaps do not think it necessary to distinguish by any prodigality of kindness a man who have formerly desert she and whom she might still suspect of serve rather for interest than affection graunt exert he rhetorical power in praise of ascham 's disinterestedness and contempt of money and declare that though he be often reproach by he friend with neglect of he own interest he never would ask any thing and inflexibly refuse all present which he office or imagine interest induce any to offer he camden however impute the narrowness of he condition to he love of dice and cockfight : and graunt forget himself allow that ascham be sometimes throw into agony by disappointed expectation it may be easily discover from he schoolmaster that he feel he want though he might neglect to supply they and we be leave to suspect that he show he contempt of money only by lose at play if this be he practice we may excuse elizabeth who know the domestick character of she servant if she do not give much to he who be lavish of a little however he might fail in he economy it be indecent to treat with wanton levity the memory of a man who share he frailty with all but whose learning or virtue few can attain and by whose excellency many may be improve while himself only suffer by he fault in the reign of elizabeth nothing remarkable be know to have befall he except that in 1563 he be invite by sir edward sackville to write the schoolmaster a treatise on education upon a occasion which he relate in the beginning of the book this work though begin with alacrity in hope of a considerable reward be interrupt by the death of the patron and afterwards sorrowfully and slowly finish in the gloom of disappointment under the pressure of distress but of the author 's disinclination or dejection there can be find no token in the work which be conceive with great vigor and finish with great accuracy and perhaps contain the best advice that be ever give for the study of language this treatise he complete but do not publish for that poverty which in we day drive author so hastily in such number to the press in the time of ascham i believe debar they from it the printer give little for a copy and if we may believe the tale of raleigh 's history be not forward to print what be offer they for nothing ascham 's book therefore lay unseen in he study and be at last dedicated to lord cecil by he widow ascham never have a robust or vigorous body and he excuse for so many hour of diversion be he inability to endure a long continuance of sedentary thought in the latter part of he life he find it necessary to forbear any intense application of the mind from dinner to bedtime and rise to read and write early in the morning he be for some year hectically feverish and though he find some alleviation of he distemper never obtain a perfect recovery of he health the immediate cause of he last sickness be too close application to the composition of a poem which he purpose to present to the queen on the day of she accession to finish this he forbear to sleep at he accustomed hour till in December 1568 he fall sick of a kind of linger disease which graunt have not name nor accurately describe the most afflictive symptom be want of sleep which he endeavor to obtain by the motion of a cradle grow every day weaker he find it vain to contend with he distemper and prepare to die with the resignation and piety of a true christian he be attend on he death-bed by gravet vicar of st sepulchre and dr nowel the learn dean of st paul 's who give ample testimony to the decency and devotion of he conclude life he frequently testify he desire of that dissolution which he soon obtain he funeral sermon be preach by dr nowel roger ascham die in the fifty-third year of he age at a time when accord to the general course of life much might yet have be expect from he and when he might have hope for much from other : but he ability and he want be at a end together and who can determine whether he be cut off from advantage or rescue from calamity he appear to have be not much qualify for the improvement of he fortune he disposition be kind and social he delight in the pleasure of conversation and be probably not much inclined to business this may be suspect from the paucity of he writings he have leave little behind he and of that little nothing be publish by himself but the toxophilus and the account of germany the schoolmaster be print by he widow and the epistle be collect by graunt who dedicate they to queen elizabeth that he might have a opportunity of recommend he son gile ascham to she patronage the dedication be not lose : the young man be make by the queen 's mandate fellow of a college in cambridge where he obtain considerable reputation what be the effect of he widow 's dedication to cecil be not know : it may be hope that ascham 's work obtain for he family after he decease that support which he do not in he life very plenteously procure they whether he be poor by he own fault or the fault of other can not now be decide but it be certain that many have be rich with less merit he philological learning would have gain he honor in any country and among we it may justly call for that reverence which all nation owe to those who first rouse they from ignorance and kindle among they the light of literature of he manners nothing can be say but from he own testimony and that of he contemporary those who mention he allow he many virtue he courtesy benevolence and liberality be celebrated and of he piety we have not only the testimony of he friend but the evidence of he writings that he english work have be so long neglect be a proof of the uncertainty of literary fame he be scarcely know as a author in he own language till mr upton publish he schoolmaster with learn note he other piece be read only by those few who delight in obsolete book but as they be now collect into one volume with the addition of some letter
